91											Actinocyhtereis microagrenon	Ayress, 1995		Fig 10, 9, 11-13		Carapace medium sized, lateral outlines subrectangular. Sexually dimorphic: males more elongated than females. Anterior margin symmetrically convex with denticulate rim. Posterior margin bluntly convex, extremity at midheight. Dorsal margin straight interrupted by a moderately well developed anterior hinge-ear, which, in the LV, bears a prominent dorsally directed spine. Ventral margin almost straight with weak oral incurvature. Subcentral tubercle low. Eye tubercle weak. Valve surface covered with dense reticulation and numerous evenly spaced spines often with common bases. One long spine occurs close to posteroventral margin. Normal pore canals simple, emergent through spines. Inner lamella moderately wide, avestibulate. Radial pore canals straight, about 35 anterior canals throughout width of inner lamella, 25 posteroventral canals. Hinge holamphidont. Muscle scars consist of a vertical row of four undivided adductors and a single “V”-shaped frontal ahead.	A subrectangular species of Actinocythereis with a fine reticulation and numerous evenly distributed spines. One prominent dorsally directed spine occurs at anterior hinge ear and one close to posteroventral margin. Anterior marginal rim and anterodorsal hinge-ear moderately well developed.	This species is most similar to Actinocythereis reticulospinosa Ayress, 1993b, from the Bortonian Stage of Hampden, South Island, but differs from that species in its lack of median ridges.		1.08*NA*0.60(holotype);0.90 to 1.06*NA*0.56 to 0.61(paratype)	Waihao district, south Canterbury, New Zealand		-44.8	171		Ashley Mudstone		Eocene	Late	Kaiatan			Outer shelf to upper slope,low-energy	mudstone	11.0	Ayress_1995_Late Eocene Ostracoda (Crustacea) from the Waihao District, South Canterbury, New Zealand.pdf

110											Copytus pseudoelongatus	Ayress, 1995		Fig 6, 9-10; Fig 13, 13		Carapace medium sized, thin shelled, and cylindrical. Anterior margin asymmetrically convex, apex just below midheight; posterior margin in RV symmetrically convex, in LV convex and very slightly truncate below mid-height. Dorsal and ventral margins parallel and straight. Inner lamella wide with slightly undulating inner margin. Anterior vestibule long and moderately narrow, posterior vestibule deep dorsally, constricted posteroventrally, narrow ventrally. Radial pore canals: about nine long anterior canals, about three long ventral canals, about 10 true, short posterior canals. Hinge adont. Subcentral muscle scars consist of a subvertical row of four closely adjacent adductors and a single subcircular frontal scar.		Diagnosis: A small, elongate species of Coptuys with a narrow anterior vestibule and a posterior vestibule that is constricted posteroventrally. Remark: This species is clearly related to Antarctic species. It differs from Coptuys elongatus Benson, 1964, found in Sulzberger Bay, Antarctica, mainly in its shorter length (C. elongatus = 0.79 mm–0.86 mm) and larger posterior vestibule. C. pseudelongatus new species differs from C. laevata (Brady, 1880), from off Heard Island, in its much smaller size (C. laevata = 0.90 mm–0.88 mm in length) and in its much narrower vestibulae.		0.70*NA*0.27	Waihao district, south Canterbury, New Zealand		-44.8	171		Ashley Mudstone		Eocene	Late	Kaiatan			Outer shelf to upper slope,low-energy	mudstone	68.0	Ayress_1995_Late Eocene Ostracoda (Crustacea) from the Waihao District, South Canterbury, New Zealand.pdf

125											Eucythere sulcocostatula	Ayress, 1995	Eucythere (Rotundracythere) sp. Ayress, 1988, p. 289, plate 8, figs. 17-22	Fig 6, 1-4		Carapace very small, moderately well calcified. Rounded subtriangular outline in lateral view, subovate in dorsal view. Anterior margin broadly rounded, less convex dorsally, sloping steeply to a high dorsal apex just ahead of mid valve length. Posterodorsal slope steeper and slightly longer, somewhat undulating, forming a shallow angle at mid valve height with short, truncate posterior margin. Ventral margin slightly concave. Sexually dimorphic: females much higher than males. LV overlaps RV around entire periphery. Carapace tumid posteroventrally, compressed anteriorly with a shallow subcentral sulcus, somewhat extended horizontally. Lateral surface ornament consists of fine L-shaped costae subparallel and close to posteroventral margin. Costa extend irregularly over the anterior surface where they unite with a very weak hexagonal reticulation. Lateral surface also weakly punctate, puncta largest subcentrally. Adductor muscle scars consist of a subvertical row of four with a U-shaped frontal scar ahead. 		A rounded subtriangular species of Eucytherura with a broadly rounded anterior margin and steeply inclined posterodorsal slope. Left valve overlaps right valve around entire periphery. Posterior half tumid, divided medially by a subhorizontal sulcus. Lateral surface ornamented with fine L-shaped costae subparallel to posteroventral margin, and sparse puncta which in the central field are moderately large. Remark: This  species is easily distinguished from most other species of the genus by virtue of its subcentral sulcus, pattern of costae and subtriangular lateral outline. 136						Krithidae			Krithe 		Krithe sp.	Ayress, 1995		Fig 5, 5-8; Fig 13, 11-12				Krithe sp. is very similar to Krithe nitida Whatley and Downing, 1983, from the Middle Miocene of Victoria, Australia, although it appears to differ from that species in having parallel dorsal and ventral margins and a rather less convex posteroventral corner. The large forms are also much larger. Krithe postcristularis McKenzie et al. 1993, from the late Eocene of Victoria, Australia, and Krithe swansoni Milhau 1993, from the early Miocene of North Island, differ from the present study material in their smaller size and internal details. Most specimens of this study fall into two groups based on size and degree of expansion of the anterior vestibule. The majority of large specimens have a distally expanded anterior vestibule, and sexual dimorphs can be recognised within each size group (see Figures 5.6–6.8, 13.11, 13.12, 14). These differences of size and vestibule possibly reflect two separate species. However, the lateral outline of each group is identical, and within the smaller group both small and distally expanded vestibule types occur in equal numbers (Figure 14). Ayress (1993) also recorded two size groupings from a Middle Eocene sample but was similarly unable to recognize consistent species-diagnostic features within each size group, and he assigned all the material to the only published taxon of the Australasian Tertiary available at that time, Krithe nitida Whatley and Downing. Differences between the two sets of Eocene material (Middle Eocene large and small forms are larger than the respective late Eocene counterparts and also differ in internal valve details) further complicate species recognition and comparison. Apart from bimodal variation in valve and the anterior vestibule size, no other features offer a basis on which to recognise species, therefore all specimens are grouped here in open nomenclature. 147											Munseyella pseudobrevis	Ayress, 1995		Fig 8, 12-14		 Carapace small, weakly inflated, outline subrectangular in dorsal and lateral views. Males more elongate than females. Anterior margin broadly convex with well-developed rim and postadjacent sulcus. Posterior margin short, truncate, weakly rimmed with two prominent denticles, one at ventral angle, the other at mid-height of valve. Dorsal margin a very gently convex shallow slope, with hinge ear in LV. Ventral margin straight. Weak dorsal marginal rim extends as posterodorsal ridge just short of posterior margin. Prominent vertical ridge extends through lower half of valve at posterior third of valve length, joins weak ventral continuation of anterior marginal rim. Two small, vertical ridges, variable in strength, usually present subcentrally. Intercostal and subcentral regions coarsely punctate. Normal pores simple. Internal features as for genus.	A species of Munseyella with a prominent anterior hinge ear in the LV, a curved posterodorsal ridge, and a vertical ridge at the posterior third of shell length that forms an L-shaped structure with ventral marginal rim. Mid-dorsal surface bears coarse punctation usually subvertically aligned.	This species is most similar to Munseyella brevis Swanson, 1979, from the Recent of New Zealand and is probably ancestral to it. M. pseudobrevis can be distinguished from that species by its more rectangular outline and symmetrical anterior margin, its strong L-shaped ventral ridge and lack of the posterodorsal tubercle structure of M. brevis.		0.36*NA*0.21(holotype);0.34 to 0.40*NA*0.18 to 0.21(paratype)	Waihao district, south Canterbury, New Zealand		-44.8	171		Ashley Mudstone		Eocene	Late	Kaiatan			Outer shelf to upper slope,low-energy	mudstone	57.0	Ayress_1995_Late Eocene Ostracoda (Crustacea) from the Waihao District, South Canterbury, New Zealand.pdf	
148											Neocytherideis reticulata	Ayress, 1995		Fig 6, 11-13		Carapace medium sized and thick shelled. Lateral outline elongate subrectangular. Anterior margin a straight steep slope with weak rim. Posterior margin strongly convex, maximum convexity at midheight, and weakly rimmed. Dorsal margin straight, subparallel with ventral margin through median third of valve length. Ventral margin straight through middle third of valve length, gently convex through posterior third, sharp incurvature at anterior third forming a distinct anterior notch. Maximum width located below midheight; carapace inflation compressed at anterior and posterior marginal regions. Shallow vertical dorsal sulcus at midlength interrupts surface ornament. Lateral surface strongly reticulate: primary reticulation with polygonal fossae, longitudinal muri dominant and aligned subparallel to dorsal margin; secondary reticulation with rounded fossae, more numerous distally. Oblique posterior ridge bisects posteroventral marginal angle. Normal pore canals evenly spaced usually emerge at small pore conuli, one conspicuous above anterior notch. Inner lamella broad with narrow long vestibula. Radial pore canals and muscle scars not visible. Hinge adont. Lateral surface ornament of primary and secondary reticulation; horizontal muri dominant and aligned subparallel to dorsal margin. Shallow dorsal sulcus present at midlength. A short inclined posterior ridge bisects posteroventral marginal angle. Remark: This species is apparently related to Neocytherideis mediata Swanson, 1969, from the early Miocene of New Zealand. 163											Patagonacythere waihaoensis 	Ayress, 1995		Fig 7, 5-8; Fig 13, 16		Carapace medium sized, moderately thick shelled, and weakly inflated. Lateral outline subrectangular, males more elongate than females. Anterior margin convex with narrow rim, and very finely denticulate. Posterior margin with narrow rim, concave dorsally, convex ventrally, with four evenly spaced denticulations. Dorsal margin straight to slightly convex with slight anterior hinge ear. Ventral margin concave in RV, straight in LV. Maximum length at midheight. Maximum height at anterior hinge ear. Normal pores sieve-type. Eye tubercle anterodorsally located with large corresponding sinus internally. Subcentral tubercle weakly developed externally, conspicuous sinus internally. Surface with T-shaped ridge at posterodorsal marginal angle, gently curved ventrolateral ridge and reticulation with polygonal fossae and slightly raised mural conjunctions. Inner lamella narrow, vestibule absent, inner margin subparallel to outer margin. Selvage weak. Radial pore canals narrow and straight, numerous, reaching outer margin, about 35 anterior canals, 20 posteroventral canals. Hinge hemiamphidont. Adductor and frontal muscle scars subdivided and closely adjacent forming an aggregate which fill subcentral pit.		Diagnosis: An elongate species of Patagonacythere with weakly developed dorsal ridge and broadly concave ventral margin. Reticulation fossae of uniform size and raised mural conjunctions. Remark: This species is similar to Patagonacythere? parvitenuis (Hornibrook) but differs from the latter species in its stronger reticulation and lack of a posterodorsal tubercle (topotypes of P.? parvitenuis are illustrated in Figures 7.9, 7.10, 7.12, 7.13 and 13.17). In its external ornament and overall shape the species is somewhat similar to Margocythera aspreta McKenzie et al., 1993, from the Eocene of Victoria and Hermanites rectidorsata Milhau, 1993 from the early Miocene of New Zealand. 174					Cytheracea	Cytherideidae	Eucytherinae		Pseudeucythere		Pseudeucythere biplana	Ayress, 1995		Fig 5, 6-9		Carapace small and thin shelled; outline subangular in lateral view, elongate subhexagonal in dorsal view with concave ends. Anterior margin strongly convex below mid height, gently so above where it extends to a dorsal arched apex at anterior third of valve length. Posterodorsal margin is straight and slopes downward to a short convex posterior margin. Ventral margin slightly convex. Sexually dimorphic: males more elongate than females. Dorsal and ventral lateral valve surfaces show weak punctae aligned along fine grooves; anterior and posterior lateral regions are smooth flat slopes which come together to form an arch, the apex of which is located at mid-length ventrally. Normal pore canals small. Inner lamella moderately broad anteriorly with crescentic vestibule, small posteroventral vestibule. Radial pore canals short, straight and simple; few in number, approximately eight anteriorly. Hinge antimordent with short terminal elements. Subcentral muscle scars consist of a subvertical row of adductors: dorsal scar large and subcircular, median two scars subrectangular and closely adjacent, posterior scar inclined and offset from other scars; and a submedian frontal scar.		An elongate species of Pseudocythere with a straight posterodorsal margin sloping to a low convex posterior margin. Anterior and posterior ends of carapace concave in dorsal aspect. Flat lateral surfaces lack ornament. This species differs from the type species, Pseudeucythere filiornata Hartmann, 1989, from Antarctic waters, mainly in its more elongate lateral outline and more angular extremities of its dorsal profile. A third species of Pseudecythere is known from the Pleistocene of the Ita Mai Tai Guyot, west Pacific.		0.40*NA*0.23	Waihao district, south Canterbury, New Zealand		-44.8	171		Ashley Mudstone		Eocene	Late	Kaiatan			Outer shelf to upper slope,low-energy	mudstone	20.0	Ayress_1995_Late Eocene Ostracoda (Crustacea) from the Waihao District, South Canterbury, New Zealand.pdf	
175											Pennyella leptodictyota	Ayress, 1995		Fig 10, 15; Fig 11. 1-3		Carapace medium sized and thick shelled, outline subrectangular in lateral view, subovate in dorsal view. Anterior margin convex with moderately narrow denticulate rim; posterior margin short, weakly pointed, maximum curvature at mid height, with denticulate rim. Dorsal margin straight and gently inclined from maximum height at anterior hinge ear to low posterior. Ventral margin weakly concave. Sexually dimorphic; males more elongate than females. Row of 8 spatulate spines occur evenly spaced along a weakly developed ventrolateral ridge. Primarily and secondarily reticulate, muri low enclosing polygonal fossae. Each mural conjunction of primary reticulation bears a short spine. Eye tubercles not prominent, somewhat obscured by surface spines and primary reticulation. Snap-knob and pit structure present at mid-length ventrally. Normal pore canals simple and evenly spaced. Inner lamella moderately wide, avestibulate. Radial pore canals narrow and straight or curved; about 35 anterior canals, 19 posterior canals. Hinge hemiamphidont. Muscle scars consist of four adductors scars in a subvertical row and a “V”-shaped frontal scar ahead.	This species is most similar to Pennyella recticulata (Ayress, 1993a) so far recorded from the Middle Eocene and Oligo-Miocene of New Zealand (Ayress, 1993a, 1993b). It differs from that species, however, in its much larger size and in its row of ventral spatulate spines.	A sighted species of Pennyella with a weak ventrolateral ridge bearing a row of 8 equally spaced, short, spatulate spines. External valve surface has weak primary and secondary reticulation; each conjunction of primary reticulation bears a short spine.		1.02*NA*0.60(holotype);1.00 to 1.09*NA*0.60 to 0.65(paratype)	Waihao district, south Canterbury, New Zealand		-44.8	171		Ashley Mudstone		Eocene	Late	Kaiatan			Outer shelf to upper slope,low-energy	mudstone	98.0	Ayress_1995_Late Eocene Ostracoda (Crustacea) from the Waihao District, South Canterbury, New Zealand.pdf

183											Taracythere conjunctispinosa	Ayress, 1995		Fig 10, 5-8		Carapace medium sized, subrectangular lateral outline. Anterior margin evenly convex with broad, crescentic, denticulate rim bordered posteriorly by a row of closely spaced low spines and post-adjacent groove. Posterior margin bluntly convex, extremity at midheight. Dorsal margin straight interrupted by prominent anterior hinge-ear. Ventral margin almost straight with weak oral incurvature. Subcentral tubercle weak. Valve external surface covered with web-like primary reticulation with associated conjunctive spines, which in posterior half, are evenly distributed. Densely secondarily reticulate. A single prominent spine occurs near margin posteroventrally. Normal pore canals simple, emerge through spines at mural conjunctions. Inner lamella narrow, vestibule absent. Radial pore canals fine, majority straight, about 32 anteriorly, about 17 posteriorly. Hinge holamphidont. Muscle scars consist of a vertical row of four undivided adductors and a “V”-shaped frontal scar. Sexually dimorphic; males more elongate than females. Ocular sinus absent.	Taracythere with web-like primary reticulation and associated, evenly distributed, spines on mural conjunctions in posterior half of carapace. Densely secondarily reticulate. Anterior marginal rim and anteodorsal hinge-ear prominent.	Taracythere conjunctispinosa differs from T. karooma (McKenzie et al., 1993) from the late Eocene of south-eastern Australia in having secondary reticulation and a conspicuous anterior hinge-ear. A closely related species, differing only in lacking secondary reticulation and having greater carapace inflation, occurs in post-Eocene sediments of New Zealand (own observations). Hirsutocythere? cf. nozokiensis Ishizaki recorded by Gurent (1993) from the late Oligocene to early Miocene of the Exmouth Plateau, off western Australia may belong to that species. 184											Taracythere hampdenensis	Ayress, 1993		Fig 9, 12				The single specimen found here is considerably smaller than Taracythere proterva (Hornibrook), has a well-smaller row of anterior marginal spines, and totally lacks secondary reticulation. 185											Taracythere proterva	Hornibrook, 1952a		Fig 9, 13; Fig 10, 1-4				The very strongly upswept posteroventral margin and concave dorsal margin are characteristic of the species. It differs from Taracythere hampdenensis (Ayress, 1993b) in its larger size, median ridge and lack of a spine on the posteroventral margin. The majority of specimens in this study (and Hornibrook’s, 1953, types) have secondary reticulation over the entire lateral valve surface. Some specimens lack such reticulation (see Figure 10.1), and also differ in having a continuous row of spines on the anterior marginal rim. A form of this species which lacked also primary reticulation has been found in the Middle Eocene, Bortonian Stage and is shown here (Figure 9.22).Comparison (see also Ayress, 1993b). Trachyleberis? sp. Milhau, 1993 from the early Miocene, Otaitanga of North Island, differs from this species in its more subtriangular outline and is similar in this respect to Trachyleberis? sp. Ayress, 1994, from the Tasman Sea, a species also here considered to belong to Taracythere. 232									Cythereis 		Cythereis? gosportensis	Blake, 1950		Plate 30, fig 23-26		"Carapace in side view elongate, subquadrate. Dorsal margin straight, subparallel to the slightly convex ventral margin. Anterior end broadly rounded, produced beyond the dorsal and ventral margins. Posterior end angular with dorsal  and ventral margins joining to form a blunt point at middle. Short, thick, blunt spines line the antero- and postero-ventral margins and several very short blunt spines appear on the ventral margin. The anterior end of carapace bears a broad, thick marginal rim, behind which is a depressed zone. A small eyespot is barely visible near the dorsal end of the anterior marginal rim. The posterior and postero-ventral areas are compressed. A narrow, prominent ridge begins below the eyespot and trends posteriorly, meeting the dorsal margin in the middle, and continues along the dorsal margin to the posterior cardinal angle where it turns ninety degrees ventrally and ends as it reaches the mid-line. A heavier and more nodular ridge lies slightly above the middle, and a fused line of heavy nodes lies just above the ventral margin. A cluster of smaller nodes appears on the antero-ventral part of the carapace within the marginal rim. Viewed from the inside the valves are moderately shallow. The marginal area is broad with the line of concrescence and the inner margin coinciding. The hinge of the right valve consists of a large, oval anterior

angle. A large, knob-like tooth occupies the posterior end of the hinge line. The hinge of the left valve consists of a large anterior socket followed by a large, blunt, arched tooth, which is attached to the dorsal margin. There is a prominent muscle scar depression in front of the center of the carapace. The muscle pattern consists of a vertical row of four scars, in front of which is another large U-shaped scar."		This species is similar to C. reklawensis Stephenson 1944, but differs in that the posterior end of C. reklawensis comes to a point well above the middle. 275									Cytherella		Cytherella excavata	Alexander, 1934	Cytherella beyrichi Jones, 1856, (non C. beyrichi Reuss, 1851), Mon. Tert. Entom. England, Paleont. Soc. London, p. 55, pl. 5, fig. 12	Plate 32, figs 3, 4; Plate 35, figs 5, 6		Female carapace oblong ovate in side view, highest slightly behind middle. Dorsal margin very gently arched. Ventral margin slightly and evenly convex downward. Anterior end broadly and evenly rounded. Posterior end broadly rounded, slightly oblique. Right valve overlaps left moderately and almost uniformly around entire periphery; overlap slightly less around anterior end and in lower half of posterior margin than elsewhere. Surface of valves coarsely and discretely punctate, and bearing a shallow, but distinct oval pit that corresponds in position and shape to the muscle-scar area on the interior of the valves. Anterior border of left valve bears a narrow marginal ridge or flange. Carapace in dorsal view widest near posterior end; anterior end subacute, posterior roundly truncated; sides of valves gently convex. Male carapace similar in outline to that of female, but with posterior end slightly lower and more oblique, and more strongly compressed. 		"C. excavata is similar in general appearance and in surface ornamentation to C. beyrichi (Reuss) from the Oligocene, Septarian clays, of Germany."" The writer has procured, through exchange with Dr. A. Franke of Arnstadt, Germany, a sample of these ""septarian clays"" from the vicinity of Berlin, as well as Oligocene clays from other localities in Germany. Specimens of C. beyrichi have been found in these samples. The carapace of this species is widest and is squarely truncated at the posterior end; in dorsal view the anterior end is blunt, and the sides of the valves flat, or even slightly concave. In lateral view the dorsal margin is arched, but the ventral margin is straight or slightly concave. C. beyrichi is also much larger than C. excavata. Several similar but distinct species have been recorded by various authors under the name C. beyrichi. Reuss' original figures of the species are very poor, and give a very erroneous conception of the specific characters. Speyer""1 has published the best figures of the true C. beyrichi. His specimens were collected from the Oligocene clays in the vicinity of Cassel, in northern Germany. The species described and figured by the same writer under the name C. transversa is undoubtedly the male of the same species. Jones and Sherborn described three species of the ""C. beyrichi group"" as occurring in the London clay (Eocene) of England. The three species are C. fabacea (Bornemann), C. beyrichi (Reuss), and C. compressa (Miinster). It is doubtful whether any of the three forms from the London clay belong to the species to which Jones and Sherborn assigned them. All three species were originally described from strata of Oligocene age in Germany. Lienenklaus has expressed the belief that the form described by Bornemann under the name C. fabacea is an immature male of C. compressa, and the present writer agrees with this interpretation. The form referred by Jones and Sherborn to C. compressa (Miinster) was originally described by Jones under the name C. londinensis, and this name may, therefore, be regarded as a valid name for the species, rather than as a synonym for C. compressa (Miinster). The form referred to C. beyrichi by the same authors appears to be very close to C. excavata, n. sp. C. excavata has been found in both the Kincaid and Wills Point formations, but at most localities is rather rare."		0.75*0.34*0.45(F); 0.67*0.3*0.45(M)	Louisiana, Texas	21			Midway											Alexander_1934_Ostracoda of the Midway (Eocene) of Texas.pdf

281						Bairdidae			Bairdia		Bairdia magna	Alexander, 1934	Bairdia magna Alexander 1927, Jour. Paleontology, vol. 1, p. 32, pl. 6, figs. 7, 8; Alexander, 1929, Texas Univ., Bull. 2907, p. 63, pl. 3, fig. 8.					This large species, which occurs frequently in exposures of the Navarro clays of the Upper Cretaceous in Texas and in equivalent strata through the Gulf Coast region, occurs also in the Midway formations. It is somewhat more common in the Kincaid formation than in the Wills point formation. This, and similar species from the Cretaceous and Tertiary, have been included by most writers under the name Bairdia subdeltoidea (Miinster). The latter species was described by Miinster from Tertiary strata exposed near Osnabruck, Germany, from Paris and Bordeaux in France, and from Castel-Arquato in Italy. Other considerations being favorable the first-named locality should be regarded as the type locality for the species. Through the courtesy of Dr. E. Jaworski, of the University of Bonn, Bonn am Rhein, Germany, the writer has procured a sample of the Upper Oligocene marine sand from Osnabruck. It has, therefore, been possible to compare the Navarro and Midway forms of B. magna Alexander with topotype specimens of B. subdeltoidea (Miinster). The two species are similar in the degree of convexity of the dorsal and ventral margins, but B. magna is higher in proportion to the length of the shell and is distinctly blunter, or less distinctly angled, at the posterior end. 287									Cythereis 		Cythereis midwayensis	Alexander, 1934		Plate 33, fig 1		Carapace oblong, highest in front. Dorsal margin straight, ventral margin nearly straight, very slightly convex downward. Anterior end broadlyrounded, rimmed, finely denticulate. Posterior end compressed, triangular in form, blunt; postero-ventral border gently convex, denticulate, postero-dorsal border slightly concave. Anterior marginal rim continues backward to about middle of dorsal margin, and there ends abruptly. A -second ridge arises, just below and free of the posterior end of the first, extends obliquely upward and backward a short distance to the dorsal margin, and continues around the entire periphery of the valve as a thick, rounded, marginal rim, joining the anterior rim at the antero-ventral angle. C. midwayensis is closely similar in both form and surface ornamentation and is obviously closely related to C. bassleri (Ulrich) from the Eocene of Maryland. Through the courtesy of Dr. R. S. Bassler, of the United States National Museum, the writer has procured locality for the species. The Maryland species is characteristically larger than the Texas Midway form, the posterior end is much shorter and more obtuse, and the surface reticulation is stronger and much coarser. Further, in C. bassleri the ventro-lateral ridge extends backward to the compressed posterior end, from which position it turns abruptly upward, nearly at right angles, and extends almost vertically, instead of obliquely, upward, joining the dorsal marginal ridge at the postero-cardinal angle. A distinct median-longitudinal ridge extends posteriorly from the subcentral tubercle in C. bassleri, and joins this nearly vertical, transverse ridge a short distance below the posterior cardinal angle. It is the writer's opinion that C. midwayensis n. sp. is the direct ancestor of C. bassleri (Ulrich), and of similar or identical forms which have been observed in strata of Wilcox and Claiborne age in Texas, Louisiana and Alabama. C. midwayensis is very abundant in nearly all outcrops of the Kincaid formation, and occurs less abundantly in most outcrops of the overlying Wills Point clays.		0.65*0.37*0.35	Louisiana, Texas	7			Midway	Kincaid; Wills Point										Alexander_1934_Ostracoda of the Midway (Eocene) of Texas.pdf

289											Cythereis prestwichiana	Jones and Sherborn, 1887	Cythereis prestwichiana Jones and Sherborn, 1887, Geol. Mag., p. 454, pl. 11, figs. lla, b; Jones and Sherborn, 1889, Suppl. Monogr. Tertiary Entom. England, Pal	Plate 32, figs 14, 15		Carapace oblong, highest in front. Dorsal and ventral margins straight, subparallel, converging slightly posteriorly. Anterior end rounded, rimmed, denticulate. Posterior end bluntly angled at about middle, bearing four or five short, stout teeth along lower margin. Continuations of the anterior marginal rim extend, ridge-like, along the dorsal and ventral borders of the valves; ventral ridge more strongly elevated than dorsal. Both ridges terminate posteriorly in sharp rectangles, immediately in front of the compressed posterior end. An obsecure subcentral node forms the anterior end of a short, narrow, median longitudinal ridge which, near the posterior tudinal ridge which, near the posterior end, turns obliquely upward to join the down-turned end of the dorsal marginal ridge. This small species is somewhat similar in appearance to C. mahonae Alexander, C. worthensis Alexander and C. austinensis Alexander, described from the Cretaceous of Texas. It differs from all three of these species, however, in the character of the reticulation which marks the valve surfaces. In C.prestwichiana the ridges of the meshes give off numerous short, sharp spurs that project into the enclosed pits, giving a distinctly lacy appearance to the reticulation. It also differs from each of these in other details of sculpture. For example, there is no subcentral node in C. mahonae nor in C. worthensis, the long, median ridges that characterize these species arising near the anterior end of the shell. The dorsal and ventral marginal ridges of C. austinensis arise within and free of the ends of the anterior marginal rim, instead of being direct continuations of it. The type figures of C. prestwichiana Jones and Sherborn do not indicate a connection between the median and dorsal ridges, such as the writer has observed in the Midway specimens. The connecting ridge in the Midway forms is often low and obscure, however, and the published figures of C. prestwichiana obviously are somewhat generalized. The Midway specimens fit both the description and figures of C. prestwichiana so perfectly in every other respect that the writer does not feel justified in naming a new species. This striking species occurs in abundance in most outcrops of both the Kincaid and Wills Point clays. 		0.60*0.28*0.33	Louisiana, Texas	21			Midway	Kincaid; Wills Point										Alexander_1934_Ostracoda of the Midway (Eocene) of Texas.pdf

295											Cytheridea fabaformis	Berry, 1925	Cytherella fabaformis Berry, 1925, Am. Jour. Sci., 5th ser., vol. 9, p. 487, fig. 13; Cytheridea fabaformis Alexander, 1929, Texas Univ., Bull. 2907, p. 76, pl. 5, fig. 18.					This strongly sculptured little species, like the above, has been recorded from the Monmouth and Navarro formations, and occurs frequently in outcrops of the basal clays of the Kincaid formation. Most Kincaid specimens are somewhat larger and more robust than those from the underlying Navarro, but are otherwise identical in every respect. A similar species has been observed in the Wilcox sandy clays outcropping near Ozark, Alabama. The Wilcox species is obviously derived from C. fabaformis (Berry), and is distinguished by the development of a high, broad, rounded, median longitudinal ridge in the posterior half of the shell, immediately above the short, shallow sulcus which characterizes both forms. 296											Cytheridea ruida	Alexander, 1934		Plate 33, fig 7		Carapace oblong, highest at anterior end. Dorsal margin straight except for slight downward slope immediately behind high antero-cardinal angle. Ventral margin nearly straight, with slight sinuosity in anterior third. Dorsal and ventral margins subparallel, converging slightly posteriorly. Anterior end rounded, strongly oblique. Posterior end obliquely truncated, with rounded, subacute postero-ventral angle. A strong, narrow ridge runs parallel to and a short distance within the anterior margin and continues backward along the ventral margin, becoming obsolete near the posterior end. A strongly elevated, narrow, elongated node, with its long axis vertically disposed, is situated about one-third of the length of the carapace from the anterior end. The lower end of this node is connected by a short, narrow, posteriorly curved ridge to the ventral marginal ridge, and the upper end gives rise to a strong, narrow ridge which extends posteriorly, slightly above the median longitudinal line of the shell, then, at a point about one-third of the length of the carapace from the posterior end, bends obliquely downward toward the postero-ventral angle and terminates near the posterior end, just above the posterior termination of the ventral ridge. A short distance behind its junction with the upper end of the vertically elongated anterior node, this longitudinal ridge gives rise to a short, narrow, ridge that extends vertically downward and terminates abruptly slightly above the middle of the ventral marginal ridge; at the point where it makes its oblique turn the longitudinal ridge gives rise to a second short ridge which extends vertically upward to the dorsal margin. Surface of valves between the ridges, rather strongly pitted. 		C. ruida is somewhat variable in degree of development of the pits that mark the valve surfaces. In some specimens, most of the pits are small and are conspicuous only along the bases of the strong ridges. In others, however, the entire surface between the ridges, and even the crests of the ridges themselves, may be strongly pitted. This strongly ornamented and distinctive species has been found only in exposures of the basal clays and glauconitic clays of the Kincaid formation. 		0.63*0.34*0.42	Louisiana, Texas	1			Midway	Kincaid										Alexander_1934_Ostracoda of the Midway (Eocene) of Texas.pdf	
297											Cytheridea ruginosa	Alexander, 1934		Plate 33, fig 9		Carapace in side view elongate, triangular ovate, highest somewhat in front of middle. Dorsal margin gently arched, nearly straight along hinge line, with rounded, obscure antero- and postero-cardinal angles. Ventral margin gently but distinctly convex downward. Anterior end broadly and evenly rounded. Posterior end with blunt, rounded postero-ventral angle. Surface of valves strongly pitted, the puncta arranged in eight to eleven roughly parallel, vertical furrows, with narrow, rounded intervening ridges;  the median two or three of these furrows are slightly deeper than the others and those near the ends of the shell become shallower and less distinct. Carapace strongly convex, widest at middle; in dorsal view, valve surfaces convex, anterior end broad, blunt, with narrow, compressed border, posterior end tapering slightly, blunt. 		C. ruginosa is somewhat similar to C. monmouthensis Berry, which has been recorded from the Monmouth formation of Maryland and the Navarro of Texas, to C. miilleri (Miinster) from the Tertiary of Europe, and to the species identified as C. mulleri by Cushman from the Eocene of Mexico33 and by Stadnichenko from the so-called Yegua (now Crockett), Eocene, of Texas.34 The writer has obtained specimens of C. monmouthensis from the Monmouth formation of Maryland, of C. mulleri from the type locality, an exposure of Oligocene clays near Osnabriick, Germany, and of the species described under the latter name by Cushman and Stadnichenko from the original collections of these writers. C. ruginosa is readily distinguished from C. monmouthensis Berry by the more elongate form and by the more numerous and more strongly developed vertical furrows with their rows of pits. In the true C. miilleri (Mtinster), the ventral margin is less convex downward, the dorsal and ventral margins are more nearly parallel, and the vertical rows of pits are fewer in number and the furrows less distinct than in C. ruginosa. In dorsal view the posterior end is much broader and more obtuse than in C. ruginosa.  Miss Stadnichenko has included two separate and distinct species under the name C. mulleri (Miinster); one of these is specifically identical with the specimens referred by Cushman to the same species, from the Eocene of Mexico, whereas the other is a peculiar form characterized by the presence of numerous small tubercles, instead of puncta, on the valve surfaces. The punctate specimens from the Moctezuma River of Mexico and from the so-called Yegua (Crockett) of Texas are similar to C. mulleri (Miinster) in having more nearly parallel dorsal and ventral margins than C. ruginosa n. sp. In dorsal view, also, the sides of the valves are flat, instead of convex, and the posterior end broad and blunt, like the anterior. They are characterized, however, by the presence of 12 to 15 vertical rows of distinct puncta, each row being situated in a deep furrow; C. miilleri exhibits even fewer and less distinct furrows than C. ruginosa. They differ further from C. ruginosa in that the furrows near the ends of the shell curve somewhat to parallel the anterior and posterior ends, rather than remaining straight and parallel to those near the center. C. ruginosa is believed to be derived from C. monmouthensis Berry, and to be ancestral to the species described as C. mulleri (Miinster) by Cushman and Stadnichenko. C. ruginosa is represented by an abundance of individuals in nearly all out- crops of the Kincaid formation and occurs in less abundance, in some outcrops of the Wills Point. The more strongly corrugated and punctate derivative of this species described by Cushman and Stadnichenko has been observed in samples collected from an outcrop of Wilcox sandy clays near Ozark, Alabama, and from numerous exposures of the Claiborne formations in Texas, Louisiana and Mississippi. 			Louisiana, Texas	6			Midway	Kincaid; Wills Point										Alexander_1934_Ostracoda of the Midway (Eocene) of Texas.pdf

Fig 3. 8-12				Bate (1972) proposed the genus Anebocythereis for the Cretaceous of Australia stressing the similarity between the type species A. amoena and Cythereis hostizeaHornibrook. Although they are indisputably different species, C. hostizea seems to fit better into the diagnosis of Anebocythereis than into the one of Cythereis Jones. Whatley and Millson (1992) proposed the genus Marwickcythereis for Eocene/Oligocene species from New Zealand, electing Cythereis marwicki Hornibrook the type species. In our opinion, however, the diagnosis of Marwickcythereis does not differ significantly from the one of Anebocythereis, and Bate’s proposal fits well for the present species. The outline, shape, ornamentation and the presence of normal pore canals in the tubercles, clearly seen in Figure11, plate 3 of Guernet(1993), led us to identify the species Henryhowella melobesioides (Brady) recorded by him as Anebocythereis hostizea (Hornibrook).		1.13 to 1.05*NA*0.66 to 0.63(RV); 1.02 to 1.16*NA*0.63 to 0.66 (LV)	Kerguelen Plateau, Indian Ocean		-60	80				Eocene	Late				deep sea		51.0	Bergue_Govindan_2010_Eocene-Pliocene deep sea ostracodes from ODP site 744A.pdf	119-744A-19H-5W-5 and 119-744A-19H-2W-5 are Late Eocene samples.

338											Cytheretta auriculata					Shape: The carapace is globular, elongated, spindle‑shaped when viewed laterally. In profile, it is almond‑shaped and fairly thin. The two valves are considered separately. Left valve: The dorsal and ventral borders are subparallel; the dorsal border is slightly arched. The anterior end, regularly rounded on the ventral side, bends sharply upward toward the dorsal border; the posterior end, serrated or not, is somewhat cut off, slanting downward toward the dorsal border. This valve, always shorter, wider, and more enveloping than the right valve, gives the individuals their globular appearance. Right valve: It is always longer and flatter than the left valve. The dorsal and ventral borders are straight and parallel. The anterior and posterior ends are narrow and rounded; a few protrusions are present at the posterior extremity. The cardinal angles are not very pronounced. Ornamentation: It is very variable depending on the specimens: some individuals are covered with very fine punctation, giving them a smooth appearance; others are strongly punctate (Pl. 1, fig. 8); the ornamentation may sometimes consist of agglomerated pits clustered at the anterior and posterior extremities and along the dorsal margin, with the ventral side lacking them; in still other individuals, the entire surface is punctate (Pl. 1, fig. 9), but the punctations are scattered and irregular: fine in the antero‑ventral region and much larger in the postero‑dorsal region.	Cytheretta auriculata seems related to Cytheretta simplex Moyes; however, in the latter, the dorsal and ventral margins are clearly parallel, while the general appearance of Cytheretta auriculata is essentially globular.			0.64*0.38*0.16(holotype); 0.65 to 0.75*0.34 to 0.38*0.15 to 0.18(paratype)		Camphon; Bois-Gouët						Eocene		Lutetian				sand	313.0	Blondeau_1972_Quelques nouvelles espèces d´Ostracodes Eocènes.pdf	In Franch
339									Cytheridea		Cytheridea atlantic 	Blondeau, 1972		Pl 2, figs 1-3		Shape: The outline is sub‑trapezoidal, with a very rounded anterior end. The posterior end is much more tapered and even displaced toward the ventral margin. The cardinal angles are not distinguishable in the convexity of the dorsal margin. A slight concavity can be observed on the ventral margin in the posterior third of the length. Ornamentation: Three large, low, rounded ridges run along almost the entire periphery of the valve. At mid‑length, a slightly marked transverse furrow crosses the valve, very finely punctate. Internal characters: They are typical of the genus; the marginal zone is well developed, with numerous straight pore canals, most often grouped in twos or threes. Sexual dimorphism: It is very weakly expressed.	Cytheretta atlantica n. sp. appears closest to Cytheridea intermedia Reuss; however, it is less arched and more finely punctate.			0.47*0.25*0.11(holotype); 0.48 to 0.52*0.25 to 0.28*0.10 to 0.15(paratype)		Camphon						Eocene		Lutetian				sandy limestone	567.0	Blondeau_1972_Quelques nouvelles espèces d´Ostracodes Eocènes.pdf	In Franch
340											Cytheridea camphonensis	Blondeau, 1972		Pl 3, figs 4, 5		Shape: Viewed laterally, the carapace displays a sub‑trapezoidal outline. The margins are nearly parallel. The anterior end curves obliquely toward the dorsal side; it bears about fifteen small denticulations on the ventral side. The posterior end, asymmetrical and dorsally truncated, curves regularly toward the ventral side. The dorsal margin is straight; the ventral margin shows a slight median concavity. The profile is oval. Ornamentation: The surface is ornamented with punctations, most often grouped in twos in concentric rows; they disappear in the anterior part. Low ridges (2 to 3), arranged between the rows of punctations, run along the anterior and ventral margins. A short transverse furrow, slightly marked, is individualized at the level of the muscle field. Internal characters: They are typical of the genus. The pores are of the cribrose type. Sexual dimorphism: very little apparent.	This species is close to Cytheridea atlantica n. sp. It differs from it by a more clearly trapezoidal outline and the presence of a transverse furrow. Nevertheless, the two species remain very similar.			0.54*0.25*0.14(holotype); 0.50 to 0.55*0.25 to 0.28*0.12 to 0.15(paratype)		Camphon						Eocene		Lutetian				sandy limestone	354.0	Blondeau_1972_Quelques nouvelles espèces d´Ostracodes Eocènes.pdf	In Franch
341									Semicytherura		Semicytherura reticulata	Blondeau, 1972		Pl 2, figs 6-10		Shape: Semi‑globular, quadrangular, of small size. Anterior end slightly smaller, but well individualized; maximum height is reached in the posterior third, at the level of strong curvature near the ventral margin. The profile is rounded, pear‑shaped; the posterior vault is elevated in the posterior zone. A roughly elliptical projection gives the posterior extremity a pointed, leaf‑shaped appearance; the profile ends in a caudal process, well individualized. Ornamentation: A reticulation system with slightly loose mesh originates near the anterior border, in front of the muscle‑scar field. One ridge escapes obliquely and runs almost horizontally along the ventral margin; another, strongly curved, joins the posterior extremity while crossing the valve diagonally. The reticulation is tight and smooth in the posteroventral region; more open in the posterior region. Inside this network one can distinguish tubercles, forming a second network in the posterior half of the valve. The ocular tubercles are barely visible. Internal characters: They are typical of the genus. The muscle‑scar field is visible on the inner side, forming four imprints preceded by the reniform imprint. Between the dorsal border and the reniform imprint appear small traces that may be attributed to accessory muscle scars. Sexual dimorphism: Very slight. Two groups of individuals can, however, be distinguished:1. Larger, more globular specimens, showing a marked gibbosity in the posteroventral region.Anterior and posterior extremities are more curved. They measure: length: 0.32 to 0.37 mm, width: 0.18 to 0.21 mm, height: 0.09 to 0.12 mm. These may represent the males. 2. Other specimens are smaller, narrower, less gibbous. They measure: length: 0.28 to 0.31 mm, width: 0.16 to 0.18 mm, height: 0.08 to 0.10 mm. These may be the females.	Semicytherura reticulata n. sp. does not resemble any known form.			0.37*0.21*0.10(holotye); 0.28 to 0.37*0.16 to 0.20; 0.08 to 0.10(paratype)		Camphon						Eocene		Lutetian				sandy limestone	264.0	Blondeau_1972_Quelques nouvelles espèces d´Ostracodes Eocènes.pdf	In Franch

Bairdia ilaroensis Reyment & Reyment; El Sogher: 295, pl. 4, figs 1–5.; 1998 Bairdoppilata ilaroensis (Reyment & Reyment); Colin, Tambareau, & Krasheninnikov: 291, pl. 1, figs 4, 5.;?2000 Bairdia ilaroensis Reyment & Reyment; Shahin:292, fig. 4.15.; 2003 Bairdia ilaroensis Reyment & Reyment; Morsi & Speijer: 70, pl. 1, fig. 5.; non 2005 Bairdia ilaroensis Reyment & Reyment; Ismail & Ied: 123, pl. 1, figs 15, 16.; non 2005 Bairdia ilaroensis Reyment & Reyment; Shahin: 755, pl. 1, figs 14, 15.; 2008 Bairdia ilaroensis Reyment & Reyment; Morsi, Faris, Zalat, & Salem: 165, pl. 1, figs 11, 13, 14.; ?2008 Bairdia ilaroensis Reyment & Reyment; Shahin, El Halaby, & El Baz: 129, pl. 1, fig. 11.	Fig. 2F-H				Bairdoppilata ilaroensis (Reyment & Reyment, 1959) was originally described from the Paleocene of Nigeria, and has been widely reported from African and Arabian regions (Bassiouni & Luger 1996). Our specimens look conspecific with two paratype specimens of Bairdoppilata ilaroensis shown in plate 1, figure 1a, b of the original description; however, the holotype looks slightly different from these paratypes in outline, being more slender (Reyment & Reyment 1959). We tentatively consider that this difference is due to sexual dimorphism. Bairdoppilata ilaroensis is similar to Bairdoppilata poddari Lubimova & Mohan in Lubimova, Guha & Mohan, 1960 reported from the Early Eocene of India (for SEM images and synonymy, see Bhandari 1996) and Bairdoppilata gliberti Keij, 1957 reported from the Middle Eocene of Belgium and France, but it is distinguished by the straight (instead of concave) dorsal half of the anterior margin. The synonym list is modified after Bassiouni & Luger (1996), Morsi et al. (2008), and Shahin et al. (2008), in which there is certain variation in outline (e.g. concave/straight dorsal half of the anterior margin) and surface ornamentation (smooth or punctate). Thus, we call our material Bairdoppilata ilaroensis sensulato, which may include the above-mentioned species from India and Europe. Indeed, Bairdoppilata gliberti [as Bairdia (Bairdoppilata) glibarti] reported in Ducasse et al. (1985, pl. 72, figs 7, 8) looks conspecific with our species. The synonymy between Bairdoppilata ilaroensis and Bairdoppilata gliberti should be carefully considered by investigating specimens from the type locality using SEM given that both species were described before SEM, was available. One of our specimens (Fig. 2F) is probably a juvenile (A-1) showing a more rounded outline compared to the adult specimen (Fig. 2G, H). Most are probably the same genus and we prefer to assign them to Phlyctenophora at least tentatively. 395						Cytheridae			Paijenborchellina		Paijenborchellina madagascarensis	Yasuhara et al., 2019		Figs 5A-K, 6A-H	Southern Madagascar, Early-Middle Eocene	Carapace well calcified, moderate in size, highest at anterodorsal corner. Outline elongate and sub-triangular in lateral view; anterior margin obliquely rounded; caudal process prominent, long, acute, down-turned, pointed ventrally; dorsal margin almost straight, dorsolateral ridge extending above dorsal margin; ventral margin almost straight or slightly concave. Anterodorsal corner angular; posterodorsal corner weakly angular. Surface primary reticulation well developed overall, except dorsomedian sulcus. Ventrolateral, median lateral, and dorsolateral ridges well developed. Ventrolateral and median lateral ridges compose an elongate loop. Dorsolateral ridge curved. Dorsomedian sulcus well developed. Terminal elements of hingement look crenulate. Other internal features not clearly observable. Sexual dimorphism distinct.	A well-calcified Paijenborchellina species with very well-developed primary reticulation and ventrolateral, median lateral, and dorsolateral ridges.	Paijenborchellina madagascarensis sp. nov. is very similar to Paijenborchellina venosa Gurney, 1979 (Paijenborchellina indoarabica Jain, 1981a is a junior synonym of Paijenborchellina venosa; see Mostafawi 2003), but is distinguished by having distinct ridges. Paijenborchellina venosa is known from very shallow and hypersaline environments with salinity >40 (Bate 1971; Al-Abdul-Razzaq et al. 1982; Mostafawi 2003). Bate (1971) considered Paijenborchellina venosa (his Paijenborchellina sp.) a salinity-tolerant (i.e. euryhaline) species showing morphological variation (e.g. development of ventrolateral ridge) under varying environmental conditions. This species also shows considerable morphological variation in the development of surface ornamentation (Figs 5, 6). Bassiouni & Luger (1996) considered Gibborchella Gründel, 1976 a junior synonym of Paijenborchellina Kusnetzova, 1957. The only difference between Paijenborchellina and Gibborchella is the presence vs absence of a ‘transverse furrow and longitudinal rib’ on the valve surface (Gründel 1976; Szczechura 1980). Gründel (1976) erected the new genus Gibborchella for the species without this structure. Both Szczechura (1980) and Bassiouni & Luger (1996) considered the difference insufficient to divide genera. This structure is only known from the type species Paijenborchellina excelens Kusnetzova, 1957, making this genus monospecific if we accept Gibborchella as a valid genus, and is rather schematically illustrated in the original description. Since no microscopic or SEM image of Paijenborchellina excelens is available, further details are uncertain, preventing comparison between Paijenborchellina and Gibborchella. However, our discovery of a second species with this structure strongly supports Szczechura’s (1980) and Bassiouni & Luger’s (1996) opinions. The elongate loop composed of ventrolateral and median lateral ridges of our species probably corresponds to the ‘transverse furrow and longitudinal rib’ of Paijenborchellina excelens. High-quality SEM images (Figs 5, 6) show clear similarity of our species with the other ‘Paijenborchellina’ species that lack this structure (i.e. Gibborchella). In particular, our species and Paijenborchellina venosa are very similar (except for this structure) and obviously congeneric. Thus, we conclude that Gibborchella Gründel, 1976 is a junior synonym of Paijenborchellina Kusnetzova, 1957.		0.663*NA*0.296(holotype); 0.764*NA*0.312(paratype); 0.747*NA*0.322(paratype)	Toliara, Madagascar	TOL4 and TOL2	-23.46 and -23.37	43.61 and 43.67				Eocene	Early-middle				shallow marine, cosatal environment	clay	112.0	Yasuharaetal2019_JSP_Madagascar_pub_w_SI. pdf

Although Bassiouni & Luger (1996) described two subspecies of Neocyprideis armata – Neocyprideis armata armata Bassiouni & Luger, 1996 and Neocyprideis armata isoarcuata Bassiouni & Luger, 1996 – we consider these to be separate species, i.e. Neocyprideis armata Bassiouni & Luger, 1996 and Neocyprideis isoarcuata Bassiouni &

Bassiouni & Luger (1996) did not compare Neocyprideis armata with Indian and Pakistani species. Neocyprideis armata is very similar to Neocyprideis chaudhuryi (Lubimova & Guha in Lubimova et al., 1960). Although the illustration of the original description of Neocyprideis chaudhuryi is not very clear, it seems that the punctate form of Neocyprideis chaudhuryi has sparse punctation according to the original description text (stating “Reticules are very infrequent”) and subsequent studies that showed microphotographic and SEM images (Guha 1961; Khosla 1978, 1988). In contrast, the punctate form of Neocyprideis armata has dense punctation. In addition, the original description of Neocyprideis chaudhuryi stated that “Surface of the valves is covered by irregularly oval angular reticules” (Lubimova et al. 1960), but Neocypri-
deis armata lacks such irregular-shaped puncta. Note that irregular-shaped punctae are not clearly visible in any illustrations, microphotographic or SEM images of Neocyprideis chaudhuryi (Guha 1961; Khosla 1978, 1988), and thus uncertainty remains. (The punctate form of) Neocyprideis armata is also very similar to Neocyprideis formosa Siddiqui, 2000, but is distinguished by its finer and less intensive punctation and by the presence of anteromarginal denticles. Neocyprideis okhaensis (Khosla, 1988) and Neocyprideis paravurensis (Khosla, 1988) are distinguished from Neocyprideis armata by having
coarser punctation and posteromarginal denticles. Thus, although Neocyprideis armata shows considerable affinity to the Indo-Pakistan species of Neocyprideis, they are separate species." 401									Hermanites		Hermanites cf. percultus	Ahmad, Neale and Siddiqui, 1991		Fig. 13C-1, 14A-D				This species is characterized by strongly prominent anterodorsal and posterodorsal corners. Similar Hermanites species in this regard are known from Tanzania (Hermanites percultus Ahmad, Neale & Siddiqui, 1991) and Europe [Hermanites orbignyana (Bosquet, 1852), Hermanites vahrenkampi dudarensis (Monostori, 1998), Hermanites vermiculata (Bosquet, 1852)]. Although the type species of Hermanites lacks the strongly prominent anterodorsal and posterodorsal corners, such intrageneric variation in outline is common in hemicytherid and thaerocytherid genera, e.g. in the well-defined genus Poseidonamicus Benson, 1972 in which several species have strongly prominent anterodorsal and posterodorsal corners showing concave dorsal margins and others have angular but not prominent dorsal corners showing straight dorsal margins resulting in a trapezoidal outline (Whatley et al. 1986; Hunt 2007).This species is very similar to and may be conspecific with Hermanites percultus. Our specimens and Hermanites percultus have an almost identical reticulation pattern and ridge arrangement, but Hermanites percultus has a better calcified carapace and better developed ridges and miri as well as a more subrectangular outline (Ahmad et al. 1991) (our specimens have a slightly more subtrian-gular appearance: Figs 13C–I, 14A–D). Thus, we prefer to call them Hermanites cf. percultus. Hermanites orbignyana (Bosquet, 1852) and Hermanites vahrenkampi dudarensis (Monostori 1998) are also very similar to this species, but have more irregular primary reticulation (Keij 1957; Ducasse et al. 1985; Monostori 1998). Hermanites vermiculata (Bosquet, 1852) is distinguished from this species by having better developed ridges and muri, and a more prominent posterior margin (Keij 1957; Ducasse et al. 1985). Bradleya? sp. B of Ahmad et al. (1991) is distinguished from this species by the position of the eye tubercle more distant from the anterodorsal margin. 404						Loxoconchidae			Loxoconcha		Loxoconcha loculus	Ahmad, Neale and Siddiqui, 1991	1991 Loxoconcha (Myrena) loculus Ahmad, Neale & Siddiqui: 245, pl. 30, figs 10–12.	Fig. 15A-J				"The rounded, short, and high outline, and flat anterior and posterior margins of Loxoconcha loculus (Ahmad, Neale & Siddiqui, 1991) show considerable similarity to Myrena Neale, 1967 (sensu Mazzini 2005) and Palmoconcha Swain & Gilby, 1974. However, we prefer to put this species in Loxoconcha Sars, 1866 because of the well-developed eye tubercle that is lacking or inconspicuous in Myrena and Palmoconcha. Although all known species of Myrena are deep-sea forms that naturally lack an eye tubercle regardless of genus, shallow-water species of a closely related genus, Kuiperiana Bassiouni, 1962, have no or inconspicuous eye tubercles. Many shallow-marine Palmoconcha species are known, including the type species, and they have no or inconspicuous eye tubercles. Internal details of our specimens are not well preserved (Fig. 15D, H).
Myrena, Kuiperiana, and Palmoconcha have only subtle known differences. All of them have a smooth (or weakly crenulate) median hinge element (Bassiouni 1962; Ishizaki & Gunther 1976; Maybury 1990; Mazzini 2005). Myrena and Kuiperiana share the same posterior terminal hinge element in the RV, i.e. one arched tooth composed of a large knob with a thin, crenulate, arched tail anteriorly, showing a ‘comma-like’ appearance (Bassiouni 1962; Maybury 1990; Mazzini 2005; Yamaguchi & Kamiya 2007). Palmoconcha has a similar, but slightly different, posterior terminal hinge element in the RV, that has more of a ‘telephone receiver-like’ appearance composed of two large knobs connected by an arched ridge (Ishizaki & Gunther 1976; Athersuch & Horne 1981; Horne & Kilenyi 1981; Horne & Whatley 1985). Comparing the external views of the type species of these genera, Kuiperiana is elongated, and Myrena and Kuiperiana are short, high, and rounded in outline; anterior and posterior flat margins are broad in Myrena and Palmoconcha, but very narrow in Kuiperiana; the caudal process is more prominent and angular in Palmoconcha than the others, and pointed at mid-height instead of above mid-height; all are covered by regular and primary reticulation, but the reticulation is comparatively more angular and irregular in Myrena and Kuiperiana and more rounded and regular in Palmoconcha; the straight part of the dorsal margin (i.e. hinge line) is shorter in Palmoconcha than in Myrena and Kuiperiana (Bassiouni 1962; Swain & Gilby 1974; Ishizaki & Gunther 1976; Uffenorde 1981; Mazzini 2005).
Many but not all researchers have considered Myrena to be a junior synonym of Kuiperiana (Maybury 1990; Ayress 1992; Mazzini 2005; Yamaguchi & Kamiya 2007). Numerous species currently assigned to Palmoconcha seem better placed in Myrena, e.g. Loxoconcha semi-
striata Kingma, 1948 and Loxoconcha parapontica Zhou, 1995, that clearly have a ‘Myrena’ external appearance and hingement (Zhao & Whatley 1989b; Zhou 1995; Yamaguchi & Kamiya 2007). There are also many species currently assigned to Kuiperiana that may be better placed in Myrena (if Myrena is not a junior synonym of Kuiperiana) (Ayress 1992; Ayress & Drapala 1993; Ayress et al. 1994). Several Kuiperiana species other than the type species do not have flat anterior and posterior margins (Whatley & Maybury 1987; Maybury 1995), showing no external similarity to Myrena despite the fact that researchers tend to consider Myrena a junior synonym of Kuiperiana (Maybury 1990; Ayress 1992). Several quadrate but short and high species are known and have been assigned to Palmoconcha, Kuiperiana and Loxoconcha (Blaszyk 1987; Szczechura 2001; Yamaguchi & Kamiya 2007), but their true generic assignment is rather uncertain. Furthermore, many species similar to Loxoconcha loculus regarding their rounded, short and high outline and flat anterior and posterior margins with and without eye-tubercle development are known from Eocene and Paleocene strata from various places in the world (Murray 1938; Huff 1970; Neale & Singh 1985; Coles & Whatley 1989; Bassiouni & Luger 1990; McKenzie et al. 1991, 1993; Ayress 1995; Monostori 1998; Ceolin et al. 2015). Their genus-level taxonomic assignments remain uncertain, despite the fact that they are important with respect to the origin of this lineage of loxoconchids. Some of these species show very similar internal (hingement) details to Myrena, Kuiperiana and Palmoconcha (Murray 1938). Given these circumstances, detailed re-study of type or topotype specimens of these three genera is needed to resolve this taxonomic problem and also to understand better the taxonomy, evolution, phylogeny and palaeobio-geography of loxoconchids. In particular, internal details are imperfectly known in the type species of these genera." S of Brouwers & Fatmi, 1992 is distinguished from the new species by its less developed ala posteriorly; less continuous and smoothly sinuous median lateral ridge; and lack of semi-vertical ridges. The new species shows obvious similarities to the type species of Neomonoceratina, Neomonoceratina columbiformis Kingma, 1948, even though it is distinguished by the ala extended below the ventral margin, two semi-vertical ridges running into the apex of the ala, and dorsolateral ridge running much closer to the dorsal margin (Keij 1979; Zhao & Whatley 1988). Although species similar to this one tend to be assigned to Paijenborchella Kingma, 1948, Paijenborchella has a long caudal process pointed ventrally (Keij 1966). It is short, upturned and pointed dorsally in Neomonoceratina Kingma, 1948 (Zhao & Whatley 1988). Thus, Neomonoceratina columbiformis and closely related species including the new species, Neomonoceratina angulosa, Neomonoceratina gujaratensis, Neomonoceratina dambarholensis and Cytheropteron sp. S of Brouwers & Fatmi, 1992 are Neomonoceratina.		0.463*NA*0.248(holotype); 0.420*NA*0.250(paratype);0.430*NA*0.242(paratype)	Toliara, Madagascar	TOL4	-23.46	43.61				Eocene	Early-middle				shallow marine, cosatal environment	clay	20.0	Yasuharaetal2019_JSP_Madagascar_pub_w_SI. pdf

Fig. 19A-D				"This species has often been included in Quadracythere or Hornibrookella but is better placed in Pokornyella which has an almond-shaped outline, poster-odorsal tubercle, and well-developed caudal process (e.g. as seen in Ducasse et al. 1985). We consider Pokornyella lamarckiana (Bosquet, 1852), Pokornyella arcana (Lubimova & Guha in Lubimova et al. 1960), and Pokornyella karkarensis (Bassiouni & Luger, 1996) conspecific. Although there is some variation in development of the posterodorsal tubercle, ridges, and primary reticulation, such variation exists even within each study or local population. A specimen reported as Jugosocythereis nishii Yamaguchi & Kamiya, 2009 (fig. 6.8) is also this species. Pokornyella arcana cornigera (Ahmad, Neale & Siddiqui, 1991) is included in this species in a broad sense, although it has better developed horizontal ridges and posterodorsal tubercle.
This species is similar to Pokornyella vanga (Ahmad, Neale & Siddiqui, 1991) but it is distinguished by the much coarser primary reticulation and more distinct sub-central tubercle. Pokornyella pseudotriangularis Morsi & Scheibner, 2009, Pokornyella sp. 2 of Morsi & Scheibner (2009), and a species reported as Hornibrookella sp. A by El Sogher (1996) are distinguished from this species by the less inflated posterior part of the carapace and finer reticulation. Pokornyella brevis Ducasse, 1963 is distinguished from this species by the less prominent anterodorsal corner, less developed posterodorsal tubercle, and more slender outline.
Pokornyella lamarckiana sensu lato (Bosquet, 1852) has a distinct morphology and has been very widely reported from Eocene strata over most of the Tethyan region in a broad sense (including Europe, Indo-Pakistan, Africa, Japan, and perhaps North America). Thus, this
species is a good biostratigraphical marker for the Eocene."			Toliara, Madagascar	TOL4	-23.46	43.61				Eocene	Early-middle				shallow marine, cosatal environment	clay	2.0	Yasuharaetal2019_JSP_Madagascar_pub_w_SI. pdf

Fig. 19E-L				"Our specimens are very similar to the type species of Reticuloquadracythere, Reticuloquadracythere apostolescui (Ducasse, 1963). They share all of the major diagnostic characters of regularly and evenly developed primary reticulation covering the entire lateral surface with high and narrow muri, inflated carapace, short and high outline, caudal process pointed below mid-height, lack of ridges other than ventrolateral and dorsolateral ridges, and long ventrolateral ridge almost continuing into the anterior margin. Thus, they are obviously congeneric and, in our opinion, conspecific in a broad sense. A comprehensive synonymy of this species can be found in Monostori (1998). Reticuloquadracythere carbonneli (Bassiouni & Luger, 1996) is probably a junior synonym of this species. Although Bassiouni & Luger (1996) indicated that Reticuloquadracythere carbonneli has a different reticulation pattern, this is not clear to us.
Investigating not only adult but also juvenile specimens will probably give better insight for solving the problem of the generic status of this genus and related genera. For example, our A-1 juveniles (Fig. 19E, G, I, J) show much better the irregular-shaped fossae and spinous muri than the adult specimen (Fig. 19L), that contrast with some similar genera such as Isalocopocythere and Hornibrookella having regularly rounded fossae and smooth muri.
Investigating juveniles of Isalocopocythere and Hornibrookella species should better clarify these differences."			Toliara, Madagascar	TOL4	-23.46	43.61				Eocene	Early-middle				shallow marine, cosatal environment	clay	16.0	Yasuharaetal2019_JSP_Madagascar_pub_w_SI. pdf

This species is very similar to Uroleberis nuda Ducasse, 1967 [D ‘Uroleberis’ nudus (sic)] (Ducasse 1967; Ducasse et al. 1985; Neale & Singh 1988), but it is distinguished by its reverse asymmetry between left and right valves. In Uroleberis paranuda sp. nov., the caudal process is better developed in the RV, and the outline is higher and slightly more angular in the RV. The same is true for the LV in Uroleberis nuda. In addition, the punctation is probably better developed in this species. The published photographic and SEM images of Uroleberis nuda are not clear enough to confirm this (Ducasse 1967; Ducasse et al. 1985; Neale & Singh 1988), but Ducasse et al. (1985) wrote that the valve surface is smooth and the SEM image shown in their paper seems to support their description. The ovate lateral outline, surface ornamentation with fossae and distinctive dorsomedian sulcus are characters shared by Hanaiborchella miurensis (Hanai, 1970) and H. triangularis (Hanai, 1970), both of which have been reported from modern coastal areas and late Cenozoic sediments in Japan (e.g., Hanai et al., 1977). H. reticularitriangularis sp. nov. differs from H. miurensis in its smaller carapace, coarser surface ornaments, and blunter muri and carinae. It is distinguished from H. triangularis by having finer surface ornaments, a narrower flattened flange along the anterior margin, and narrower horizontal carinae. H. ikeyai (Irizuki and Yamada in Irizuki et al., 2004) is another related species. This species was originally described as a species in the genus Neomonoceratina Kingma, 1948, but is reassigned to Hanaiborchella Gru ̈ ndel, 1976 based on the presence of a short caudal process in the middle of the carapace. The new species differs from H. ikeyai by its coarser reticulation and blunt carinae.	shallow water species	0.43*NA*0.23(holotype, M); 0.43*NA*0.23(paratype, M, carapace); 0.44*NA*0.25(paratype, M, carapace); 0.41*NA*0.24(paratype, F, carapace)		ATIW07; ATIW08; ESM	34.57	134.97	Kobe	Iwaya		Eocene	Middle-late				shallow marine, inner shelf	muddy fine-grained sandstone with mud-pipe	80.0	Yamaguchi_Matsubara_Kamiya_2005_Eocene ostracodes from the Iwaya Formation on Awajishima Island 1.pdf	middle latitudes of the northwestern Pacific.

The new species differs, however, from M. hatatatensis, originally described from the Miocene Hatatate Formation of Miyagi Prefecture in northeastern Japan, by its reticulation along the anterior margin, concentric muri, and the lack of a blunt ventral carina and a carina in the posterodorsal area. M. kiklukhensis, which was reported from Holocene sediments off Alaska and originally described as a member of the genus Pectocythere Hanai, 1957, belongs to Munseyella based on the rectangular lateral outline with the posterior margin bearing two short spines and the amphidont hingement of the genus Munseyella [1⁄4 pentodont hingement of Hanai, 1957, 1961] as shown by Hanai (1957, 1961). Surface ornament with reticulation. Reticulation formed by rectanglar to elongate polyogonal fossae. Muri oriented parallel to anterior and ventral margins prominent relative to other muri. Four muri running along anterior and ventral areas are particularly distinct. Distinct muri on central area obliquely stretched posterodorsalward. Eye tubercle prominent. Marginal denticles attached to anterior margin. Sexual dimorphism distinct: length of male larger than that of female.
Dorsal outline subovate: anterior and posterior ends angular; lateral margins asymmetrically curved. Maximum width across posterior of valve."	Robertsonites characterized by subrectangular lateral outline and surface ornament with rectanglar to elongate polyogonal fossae, four distinct muri along anterior and ventral margins and oblique muri on central area.	Reticulation with distinct horizontal muri on the anterior and ventral areas is shared with R. tsugaruanus Tabuki, R. reticulatus Irizuki and Yamada, and R.? sp. of Yamaguchi and Kamiya (2007a). R. tsugaruanus was originally described from Plio-Pleistocene strata of northeastern Japan (Tabuki, 1986), whereas R. reticulatus was described from Miocene strata of Central Japan (Irizuki et al., 2004). R. ? sp. was reported from Oligocene strata in Kyushu (Yamaguchi and Kamiya, 2007a). This new species is distinguished from R. tsuruganus and R. reticulatus by longer carapace and rectangular and elongate-polygonal fossae in reticulation (Fig. 5). It differs from R. ? sp. by having elongate polygonal and relatively coarse fossae.		"1.10*0.41*0.56(holotype);

434			Platycopida		Cytherelloidea	Cytherellidae			Cytherelloidea		Cytherelloidea squiresi	Yamaguchi and Goedert, 2009		Pl. 1, figs 1-6	CSUN loc. 1563.	"Carapace slightly robust and medium sized. Lateral outline elliptical: anterior and posterior margins round; dorsal margin straight or slightly sinuous; ventral margin curved and concave near middle. Maximum length across middle. Maximum height across anterodorsal corner.
Surface ornamented with two horizontal carinae, irregular reticulation and narrow marginal rim. The carinae parallel each other and are curved convexly near middle. Upper carina across central area; lower carina on ventral area. Reticulation present between lower carina and ventral margin and formed by irregular fossae. Posterior marginal rim developed. Marginal denticles project from posterior margin.
Internally, sinuate hingement line. Hingement adont-type. In left valve, socket on median element.

Right valve dorsally overlapped by left."	A Cytherelloidea ornamented with two horizontal carinae, a narrower anterior marginal rim and characterized by a flattened posterior end in the dorsal view.	This new species resembles Cytherelloidea californica Le Roy, 1943 and C. directiangular Holden, 1964 in the lateral outline and two horizontal carinae. Cytherelloidea californica and C. directiangular were originally described from the Middle Pleistocene Lomita Marl Member of the San Pedro Formation and the Upper Cretaceous Rosario Formation in California, respectively. Cytherelloidea squiresi is smaller than either of the two species from California. Cytherelloidea squiresi is also distinguishable from C. directiangular by having undeveloped marginal rims and differs from C. californica in having narrower carinae in the lateral view, and a narrower and distinct anterior marginal rim and flattened posterior end in dorsal view. A Bairdoppilata characterized by slightly high carapace with round anterior margin and caudal process below middle of posterior margin and nine muscle-scars within left valve.	Bairdoppilata crescentana sp. nov. resembles B. cratericola Maddocks, 1969 and B. cretacea (van Veen, 1936) in lateral outline, especially outlines of the anterior and posterior margins. Bairdoppilata cratericola was originally described on the basis of living specimens from Madagascar and B. cretacea (van Veen, 1936) was found in European Cretaceous strata and the Upper Cretaceous Rosario Formation of California (Holden, 1964). Bairdoppilata crescentana can be distinguished from B. cratericola (size range of single valve: L=0.88–0.89 mm, H=0.50–0.59 mm; Maddocks, 1969) by having a larger carapace and one muscle-scar more than the modern species. Bairdoppi- lata crescentana differs from B.cretacea (size range of single valve: L=0.88–0.92 mm, H=0.45–0.65 mm, W=0.21–0.27 mm; Holden, 1964) in having a wider and slightly higher carapace and two muscle-scars less than the Cretaceous species. 463											Neonesidea? sp. B			Figure 5.4-5.7		Carapace robust and large. Because of the uncertainty of the morphology of this species’ hinge, it cannot be determined if it should be attributed to Neonesidea or Bairdoppilata Coryell, Sample, and Jennings, 1935. According to Maddocks (1969), the lateral outline of Neonesidea is elongate and subtriangular, with the apex of the anterior margin above that of the posterior margin. The lateral outline of many species of Bairdoppilata, however, is rounded sub-hexagonal, with the apex of the anterior margin at or nearly at the same level of the apex of the posterior margin. Based on the lateral outline, this species is tentatively assigned to the genus Neonesidea. This species is similar to N. schulzi (Hartmann, 1964) and N. spica Holden, 1976 in lateral outline, which were originally described from living specimens of Madagascar and Miocene specimens of Midway Island, respectively. The external views alone are insufficient to identify these species down to the species level. This species is distinguished from N. boninensis n. sp. by having an asymmetric curve to the ventral margin and a non-flattened ventral surface. It differs from N. sp. A by more slender posterior area in dorsal view and by its symmetrical lateral margins in anterior view.		"0.82*0.42*0.54(carapase);

Muri stretch vertically on posterior half and converge on alae. Anterior half mostly smooth, with some faveolate ornaments. Antero- and posterodorsal margins with broad, flattened flanges. Sulcus in central area. Eye tubercle not observed. Right valve overlaps left valve dorsally. 
In dorsal view, carapace subtrapezoidal with sharp anterior and posterior ends. Shallow indents located at middle of carapace. Alae spread like a fan."	Carapace small and robust. Subtrapezoidal lateral outline. Caudal process developed. Anterior and posterior margins with broad, flattened flanges. Alae spread below ventral margin in lateral view and fan-shaped in dorsal view. Surface ornaments with fossae and wrinkled carinae.	This species is clearly distinguished from Cytheropteron miurense, which is found in Miocene to modern sediments around Japan (e.g., Hanai et al., 1977), by its curved posterodorsal corner, coarser reticulation with rectangular fossae, anterior and posterior margins with broader flattened flanges, and alae projecting below the ventral margin. It resembles Oculocytheropteron aviformum McKenzie, Reyment, and Reyment, 1993, from the Eocene Browns Creek Clay Formation in Victoria, Australia, in lateral outline, distinct projecting alae, and in having the right valve overlapping the left valve, but differs from it by the coarser reticulation formed by the broader muri and rectangular fossae on the posterior and central areas, and the lack of eye tubercles.		0.47*0.38*0.24(carapase);																Yamaguchi_2006_EoceneOstracodaSouthwestJapan.pdf

494				Podocopina 	Cypridacea	Paracyprididae			Phlyctenophora		Phlyctenophora sp.			Pl. 1: 9, 10		Sub−lunate in side view, rather evenly andmarkedly laterally inflated, narrowly rounded a teriorly, more so posteroventrally, arched dorsally, highest in the middle, weakly concave ventrally. The left valve larger than right, overlapping the latter mostly anterodorsally		All these features seem typical of Phlyctenophora, a genus described by Brady 1880, based on material found by him during the Challenger Expedition in the Southern Pacific. In comparison with Phlyctenophora zealandica Brady, 1880, the type species, as figured (only internally) by Yassini et al. (1993), from the Gulf of Carpentaria (northwestern Australia), the present specimens are much smaller, and more triangular in lateral view. The present species differ in details from all other species of this genus, especially those from the Neogene of Europe. It is possible that Paracypris? sp., described by Kielbowicz (1988) from the Late Oligocene–Early Miocene of Argentina (Austral Basin) should also be referred to Phlyctenophora. In comparison with this latter species, the present species is smaller and differs in outline and length to height ratio.
